							<div class="mw-indicators">
		<div id="mw-indicator-pp-default" class="mw-indicator"><div class="mw-parser-output"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Protection_policy#semi" title="This article is semi-protected."><img alt="Page semi-protected" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/20px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="20" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/40px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></a></span></div></div>

					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the bicycle-like watercraft, see <a href="/wiki/Hydrocycle" title="Hydrocycle">hydrocycle</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Biogeochemical cycle for movement of water on Earth</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:USGS_WaterCycle_English_ONLINE_20221013.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/USGS_WaterCycle_English_ONLINE_20221013.png/500px-USGS_WaterCycle_English_ONLINE_20221013.png" decoding="async" width="340" height="233" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/USGS_WaterCycle_English_ONLINE_20221013.png/960px-USGS_WaterCycle_English_ONLINE_20221013.png 2x" data-file-width="11400" data-file-height="7800" /></a><figcaption>A detailed diagram depicting the global water cycle. The direction of movement of water between reservoirs tends towards upwards movement through <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a> and downward movement through <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. The diagram also shows how human water use impacts where water is stored and how it moves.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>The <b>water cycle</b> (or <b>hydrologic cycle</b> or <b>hydrological cycle</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical cycle</a> that involves the continuous change in form of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> on, above and below the surface of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> across different reservoirs. The mass of water on Earth remains fairly constant over time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the partitioning of the water into the major reservoirs of <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">salt water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheric water</a> is variable and depends on <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climatic variables</a>. The water moves from one reservoir to another, such as from river to <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a>, or from the ocean to the atmosphere due to a variety of physical and chemical processes. The processes that drive these movements, or <a href="/wiki/Flux_(biology)" title="Flux (biology)">fluxes</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infiltration_(hydrology)" title="Infiltration (hydrology)">infiltration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">surface runoff</a>, and subsurface flow. In doing so, the water goes through different phases: liquid, solid (<a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">vapor</a>. The ocean plays a key role in the water cycle as it is the source of 86% of global evaporation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The water cycle is driven by energy exchanges in the form of heat transfers between different phases. The energy released or absorbed during a phase change can result in <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> changes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Heat is absorbed as water transitions from the liquid to the vapor phase through evaporation. This heat is also known as the latent heat of vaporization.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Conversely, when water condenses or melts from solid ice it releases energy and heat. On a global scale, water plays a critical role in transferring heat from the tropics to the poles via ocean circulation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The evaporative phase of the cycle also acts as a purification process by separating water molecules from salts and other particles that are present in its liquid phase.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The condensation phase in the atmosphere replenishes the land with freshwater. The flow of liquid water transports minerals across the globe. It also reshapes the geological features of the Earth, through processes of weathering, <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>, and deposition. The water cycle is also essential for the maintenance of most life and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> on the planet.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">Human actions</a> are greatly affecting the water cycle. Activities such as <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a>, and the extraction of <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> are altering natural landscapes (<a href="/wiki/Land_use_change" class="mw-redirect" title="Land use change">land use changes</a>) all have an effect on the water cycle.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:22&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1153">&#58;&#8202;1153&#8202;</span></sup> On top of this, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> is leading to an <a href="/wiki/Intensification_of_the_water_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Intensification of the water cycle">intensification of the water cycle</a>. Research has shown that global warming is causing shifts in precipitation patterns, increased frequency of <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">extreme weather</a> events, and changes in the timing and intensity of rainfall.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 85">&#58;&#8202;85&#8202;</span></sup> These water cycle changes affect <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity" title="Water scarcity">water availability</a>, agriculture, and human societies.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="text-align:center;font-size:100%;font-weight:bold;;color: var(--color-base)"><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Water cycle</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><div class="hlist">

</div></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="text-align:center;font-size:100%;font-weight:bold;;color: var(--color-base)"><a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Carbon cycle</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><div class="hlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Global</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_carbon_cycle" title="Atmospheric carbon cycle">atmospheric</a></li>

<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Earth%27s_Water_Cycle.ogv/250px--Earth%27s_Water_Cycle.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" height="141" data-durationhint="353" data-mwtitle="Earth&#39;s_Water_Cycle.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Earth%27s_Water_Cycle.ogv"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Earth%27s_Water_Cycle.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&quot;theora, vorbis&quot;" data-width="640" data-height="360" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/93/Earth%27s_Water_Cycle.ogv/Earth%27s_Water_Cycle.ogv.144p.mjpeg.mov" type="video/quicktime" data-transcodekey="144p.mjpeg.mov" data-width="256" data-height="144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/93/Earth%27s_Water_Cycle.ogv/Earth%27s_Water_Cycle.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /><track src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3AEarth%27s_Water_Cycle.ogv&amp;lang=eu&amp;trackformat=vtt&amp;origin=%2A" kind="subtitles" type="text/vtt" srclang="eu" label="euskara ‪(eu)‬" data-dir="ltr" /></video></span><figcaption>Video of the Earth's water cycle (NASA)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Overall_process">Overall process</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Water_distribution_on_Earth" title="Water distribution on Earth">Water distribution on Earth</a></div>
<p>The water cycle is powered by the energy emitted from the sun. There are several ways in which this is accomplished, one of the first ways is through <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> where the energy from the sun heats the water in oceans, lakes, streams, rivers, seas, ponds, etc. and that water goes through a phase change to become a gas (water vapor) that goes up into the atmosphere. Two other ways that water gets into the atmosphere is through snow and ice <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimating</a> into water vapor and through <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a> which is water <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpired</a> from plants and evaporated from the soil.
</p><p>Clouds form because water molecules have a smaller molecular mass than the major gas components of the atmosphere (oxygen, O2; and nitrogen, N2); this smaller molecular mass leads to water having a lower density which drives the water molecules higher up in the atmosphere due to <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyancy</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/Gas_laws" title="Gas laws">as altitude increases, air pressure decreases which causes a drop in temperature</a>. The lower temperature forces the water vapor to go through another phase change, this time it forces it to <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condense</a> into liquid water droplets which are supported by an updraft; if there is enough of these water droplets over a large area, it is considered a <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">cloud</a>. Condensation of the water vapour closer to the ground level is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">Atmospheric circulation</a> moves water vapor around the globe; cloud particles collide, grow, and fall out of the upper atmospheric layers as <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>. Some precipitation falls as snow, hail, or sleet, and can accumulate in <a href="/wiki/Ice_cap" title="Ice cap">ice caps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>, which can store frozen water for thousands of years. Most water falls as rain back into the ocean or onto land, where the water flows over the ground as <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">surface runoff</a>. A portion of this runoff enters rivers, with streamflow moving water towards the oceans. Runoff and water emerging from the ground (<a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a>) may be stored as freshwater in lakes. Not all runoff flows into rivers; much of it soaks into the ground as <a href="/wiki/Infiltration_(hydrology)" title="Infiltration (hydrology)">infiltration</a>. Some water infiltrates deep into the ground and replenishes <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>, which can store freshwater for long periods of time. Some infiltration stays close to the land surface and can seep back into surface-water bodies (and the ocean) as groundwater discharge or be taken up by plants and transferred back to the atmosphere as water vapor by <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a>. Some groundwater finds openings in the land surface and emerges as freshwater springs. In river valleys and <a href="/wiki/Floodplain" title="Floodplain">floodplains</a>, there is often continuous water exchange between surface water and ground water in the <a href="/wiki/Hyporheic_zone" title="Hyporheic zone">hyporheic zone</a>. Over time, the water returns to the ocean, to continue the water cycle.
</p><p>The ocean plays a key role in the water cycle. The ocean holds "97% of the total water on the planet; 78% of global precipitation occurs over the ocean, and it is the source of 86% of global evaporation".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:HydrologicalCycle1.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HydrologicalCycle1.png/500px-HydrologicalCycle1.png" decoding="async" width="450" height="328" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HydrologicalCycle1.png/960px-HydrologicalCycle1.png 2x" data-file-width="1551" data-file-height="1130" /></a><figcaption> <div class="center">Processes leading to movements and phase changes in water</div></figcaption></figure>
<p>Important physical processes within the water cycle include (in alphabetical order):
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Advection" title="Advection">Advection</a>: The movement of water through the atmosphere.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Without advection, water that evaporated over the oceans could not precipitate over land. <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_river" title="Atmospheric river">Atmospheric rivers</a> that move large volumes of water vapor over long distances are an example of advection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">Condensation</a>: The transformation of water vapor to liquid water droplets in the air, creating <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">clouds</a> and fog.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">Evaporation</a>: The transformation of water from liquid to gas phases as it moves from the ground or bodies of water into the overlying atmosphere.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The source of energy for evaporation is primarily <a href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a>. Evaporation often implicitly includes <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a> from <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, though together they are specifically referred to as <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>. Total annual evapotranspiration amounts to approximately 505,000&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (121,000&#160;cu&#160;mi) of water, 434,000&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (104,000&#160;cu&#160;mi) of which evaporates from the oceans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-The&#95;Water&#95;Cycle&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Water_Cycle-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 86% of global evaporation occurs over the ocean.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Infiltration_(hydrology)" title="Infiltration (hydrology)">Infiltration</a>: The flow of water from the ground surface into the ground. Once infiltrated, the water becomes <a href="/wiki/Soil_moisture" title="Soil moisture">soil moisture</a> or groundwater.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A recent global study using water stable isotopes, however, shows that not all soil moisture is equally available for <a href="/wiki/Groundwater_recharge" title="Groundwater recharge">groundwater recharge</a> or for plant transpiration.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Percolation" title="Percolation">Percolation</a>: Water flows vertically through the soil and rocks under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">Precipitation</a>: Condensed water vapor that falls to the Earth's surface. Most precipitation occurs as <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>, but also includes <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fog_drip" title="Fog drip">fog drip</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graupel" title="Graupel">graupel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ice_pellets" title="Ice pellets">sleet</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Approximately 505,000&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (121,000&#160;cu&#160;mi) of water falls as precipitation each year, 398,000&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (95,000&#160;cu&#160;mi) of it over the oceans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-The&#95;Water&#95;Cycle&#95;15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Water_Cycle-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-snow&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snow-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The rain on land contains 107,000&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (26,000&#160;cu&#160;mi) of water per year and a snowing only 1,000&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (240&#160;cu&#160;mi).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-snow&#95;20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snow-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 78% of global precipitation occurs over the ocean.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Runoff_(hydrology)" title="Runoff (hydrology)">Runoff</a>: The variety of ways by which water moves across the land. This includes both surface runoff and <a href="/wiki/Channel_runoff" class="mw-redirect" title="Channel runoff">channel runoff</a>. As it flows, the water may seep into the ground, evaporate into the air, become stored in lakes or reservoirs, or be extracted for agricultural or other human uses.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Subsurface_flow" title="Subsurface flow">Subsurface flow</a>: The flow of water underground, in the <a href="/wiki/Vadose_zone" title="Vadose zone">vadose zone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aquifers" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquifers">aquifers</a>. Subsurface water may return to the surface (e.g. as a spring or by being pumped) or eventually seep into the oceans. Water returns to the land surface at lower elevation than where it infiltrated, under the force of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> or gravity induced pressures. Groundwater tends to move slowly and is replenished slowly, so it can remain in aquifers for thousands of years.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">Transpiration</a>: The release of water vapor from plants and soil into the air.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Residence_times">Residence times</h3></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin-left:1em; text-align:center" align="right">
<caption>Average reservoir residence times<sup id="cite&#95;ref-PHYS&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PHYS-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th style="text-align:left">Reservoir</th>
<th>Average residence time
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left">Antarctica
</td>
<td>20,000 years
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left">Oceans
</td>
<td>3,200 years
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left">Glaciers
</td>
<td>20 to 100 years
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left">Seasonal snow cover
</td>
<td>2 to 6 months
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left">Soil moisture
</td>
<td>1 to 2 months
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left">Groundwater: shallow
</td>
<td>100 to 200 years
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left">Groundwater: deep
</td>
<td>10,000 years
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left">Lakes (see <a href="/wiki/Lake_retention_time" title="Lake retention time">lake retention time</a>)
</td>
<td>50 to 100 years
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left">Rivers
</td>
<td>2 to 6 months
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left">Atmosphere
</td>
<td>9 days
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Residence_time_(fluid_dynamics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Residence time (fluid dynamics)">residence time</a></i> of a reservoir within the hydrologic cycle is the average time a water molecule will spend in that reservoir (<i>see table</i>). It is a measure of the average age of the water in that reservoir.
</p><p>Groundwater can spend over 10,000 years beneath Earth's surface before leaving.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Particularly old groundwater is called <a href="/wiki/Fossil_water" title="Fossil water">fossil water</a>. Water stored in the soil remains there very briefly, because it is spread thinly across the Earth, and is readily lost by evaporation, transpiration, stream flow, or groundwater recharge. After evaporating, the residence time in the atmosphere is about 9 days before condensing and falling to the Earth as precipitation.
</p><p>The major ice sheets – <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> – store ice for very long periods. Ice from Antarctica has been reliably dated to 800,000 years before present, though the average residence time is shorter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In hydrology, residence times can be estimated in two ways.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The more common method relies on the principle of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_mass" title="Conservation of mass">conservation of mass</a> (<a href="/wiki/Water_balance" title="Water balance">water balance</a>) and assumes the amount of water in a given reservoir is roughly constant. With this method, residence times are estimated by dividing the volume of the reservoir by the rate by which water either enters or exits the reservoir. Conceptually, this is equivalent to timing how long it would take the reservoir to become filled from empty if no water were to leave (or how long it would take the reservoir to empty from full if no water were to enter).
</p><p>An alternative method to estimate residence times, which is gaining in popularity for dating groundwater, is the use of <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopic</a> techniques. This is done in the subfield of <a href="/wiki/Isotope_hydrology" title="Isotope hydrology">isotope hydrology</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Water_in_storage">Water in storage</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">Water resources</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_distribution_on_Earth" title="Water distribution on Earth">Water distribution on Earth</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:HumanIntegratedWaterCycle_(2).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/HumanIntegratedWaterCycle_%282%29.jpg/500px-HumanIntegratedWaterCycle_%282%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="500" height="291" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/HumanIntegratedWaterCycle_%282%29.jpg/1280px-HumanIntegratedWaterCycle_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2997" data-file-height="1747" /></a><figcaption>Water cycle showing human influences and major pools (storages) and fluxes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>The water cycle describes the processes that drive the movement of water throughout the <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>. However, much more water is "in storage" (or in "pools") for long periods of time than is actually moving through the cycle. The storehouses for the vast majority of all water on Earth are the oceans. It is estimated that of the 1,386,000,000&#160;km<sup>3</sup> of the world's water supply, about 1,338,000,000&#160;km<sup>3</sup> is stored in oceans, or about 97%. It is also estimated that the oceans supply about 90% of the evaporated water that goes into the water cycle.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Earth's ice caps, glaciers, and permanent snowpack store another 24,064,000&#160;km<sup>3</sup>, accounting for only 1.7% of the planet's total water volume. However, this quantity of water is 68.7% of all fresh water on the planet.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Changes_caused_by_humans">Changes caused by humans</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Land_cover_and_land_use_changes">Land cover and land use changes</h3></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Natural_%26_impervious_cover_diagrams_EPA.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Natural_%26_impervious_cover_diagrams_EPA.jpg/500px-Natural_%26_impervious_cover_diagrams_EPA.jpg" decoding="async" width="400" height="193" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/46/Natural_%26_impervious_cover_diagrams_EPA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="762" data-file-height="367" /></a><figcaption>Relationship between <a href="/wiki/Impervious_surfaces" class="mw-redirect" title="Impervious surfaces">impervious surfaces</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">surface runoff</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Human activities can alter the water cycle at the local or regional level. This happens due to <a href="/wiki/Land_development" title="Land development">changes in land use</a> and <a href="/wiki/Land_cover" title="Land cover">land cover</a>. Such changes affect "precipitation, evaporation, flooding, groundwater, and the availability of freshwater for a variety of uses".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:22&#95;8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1153">&#58;&#8202;1153&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>Examples of common land use changes include urbanization, agricultural expansion, and deforestation. These changes can increase soil compaction and impervious surface cover which decrease the infiltration capacity of soils and result in greater surface runoff rates.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Deforestation has local and regional effects; at the local level it reduces soil moisture, evaporation, rainfall, and snowfall; at the regional level it can cause temperature changes that affect that affect rainfall patterns.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:22&#95;8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1153">&#58;&#8202;1153&#8202;</span></sup> 
</p><p>Water management structures such as dams, stormwater drains, and sewage pipes can also alter local hydrologic conditions. Dams can alter natural flow rates, decrease water quality, and lead to a loss of habitat for aquatic species.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Stormwater drains function to decrease runoff rates, regulate flow rates, and increase groundwater recharge.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Leakage from sewage pipes may artificially contribute to groundwater recharge, resulting in higher stream baseflow conditions and groundwater contamination.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Groundwater_depletion" title="Groundwater depletion">Groundwater depletion</a>, however, remains an ongoing concern as groundwater is being pumped at unsustainable rates to meet municipal, industrial, and agricultural water demands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Water_cycle_intensification_due_to_climate_change">Water cycle intensification due to climate change</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle" title="Effects of climate change on the water cycle">Effects of climate change on the water cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_oceans" title="Effects of climate change on oceans">Effects of climate change on oceans</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:20211109_Frequency_of_extreme_weather_for_different_degrees_of_global_warming_-_bar_chart_IPCC_AR6_WG1_SPM.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/20211109_Frequency_of_extreme_weather_for_different_degrees_of_global_warming_-_bar_chart_IPCC_AR6_WG1_SPM.svg/250px-20211109_Frequency_of_extreme_weather_for_different_degrees_of_global_warming_-_bar_chart_IPCC_AR6_WG1_SPM.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="141" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/20211109_Frequency_of_extreme_weather_for_different_degrees_of_global_warming_-_bar_chart_IPCC_AR6_WG1_SPM.svg/500px-20211109_Frequency_of_extreme_weather_for_different_degrees_of_global_warming_-_bar_chart_IPCC_AR6_WG1_SPM.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="675" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">Extreme weather</a> (heavy rains, <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">droughts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heat_wave" title="Heat wave">heat waves</a>) is one consequence of a <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle" title="Effects of climate change on the water cycle">changing water cycle due to global warming</a>. These events will be progressively more common as the Earth warms more and more.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Figure SPM.6">&#58;&#8202;Figure SPM.6&#8202;</span></sup></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Soil_moisture_and_climate_change.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Soil_moisture_and_climate_change.svg/250px-Soil_moisture_and_climate_change.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="197" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Soil_moisture_and_climate_change.svg/500px-Soil_moisture_and_climate_change.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="755" /></a><figcaption>Predicted changes in average soil moisture for a scenario of 2°C global warming. This can disrupt agriculture and ecosystems. A reduction in soil moisture by one <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviation</a> means that average soil moisture will approximately match the ninth driest year between 1850 and 1900 at that location.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Since the middle of the 20th century, human-caused <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> has resulted in observable changes in the global water cycle.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 85">&#58;&#8202;85&#8202;</span></sup> The <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Sixth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Sixth Assessment Report">IPCC Sixth Assessment Report</a> in 2021 predicted that these changes will continue to grow significantly at the global and regional level.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 85">&#58;&#8202;85&#8202;</span></sup> These findings are a continuation of scientific consensus expressed in the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fifth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fifth Assessment Report">IPCC Fifth Assessment Report</a> from 2007 and other special reports by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> which had already stated that the water cycle will continue to intensify throughout the 21st century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:22&#95;8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle" title="Effects of climate change on the water cycle">Effects of climate change on the water cycle</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle" title="Effects of climate change on the water cycle">effects of climate change on the water cycle</a> are profound and have been described as an <i>intensification</i> or a <i>strengthening</i> of the water cycle (also called the hydrologic cycle).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Effects&#95;of&#95;climate&#95;change&#95;on&#95;the&#95;water&#95;cycle&#95;:22&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle_:22-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1079">&#58;&#8202;1079&#8202;</span></sup> This effect has been observed since at least 1980.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Effects&#95;of&#95;climate&#95;change&#95;on&#95;the&#95;water&#95;cycle&#95;:22&#95;36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle_:22-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1079">&#58;&#8202;1079&#8202;</span></sup> One example is when heavy rain events become even stronger. The effects of climate change on the water cycle have important negative effects on the availability of <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">freshwater</a> resources, as well as other water reservoirs such as <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheets</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soil_moisture" title="Soil moisture">soil moisture</a>. The water cycle is essential to life on Earth and plays a large role in the global <a href="/wiki/Climate_system" title="Climate system">climate system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ocean_circulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocean circulation">ocean circulation</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">warming of our planet</a> is expected to be accompanied by changes in the water cycle for various reasons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Effects&#95;of&#95;climate&#95;change&#95;on&#95;the&#95;water&#95;cycle&#95;:2&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle_:2-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, a warmer atmosphere can contain more water vapor which has effects on <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">rainfall</a>.   
</p><p>The underlying cause of the intensifying water cycle is the increased amount of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> in the atmosphere, which lead to a warmer atmosphere through the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Effects&#95;of&#95;climate&#95;change&#95;on&#95;the&#95;water&#95;cycle&#95;:2&#95;37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle_:2-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fundamental laws of physics explain how the <a href="/wiki/Saturation_vapor_pressure" class="mw-redirect" title="Saturation vapor pressure">saturation vapor pressure</a> in the atmosphere increases by 7% when temperature rises by 1&#160;°C.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Effects&#95;of&#95;climate&#95;change&#95;on&#95;the&#95;water&#95;cycle&#95;:1&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle_:1-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This relationship is known as the <a href="/wiki/Clausius-Clapeyron_Equation" class="mw-redirect" title="Clausius-Clapeyron Equation">Clausius-Clapeyron equation</a>. 
</p><p>The strength of the water cycle and its changes over time are of considerable interest, especially as the climate changes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Effects&#95;of&#95;climate&#95;change&#95;on&#95;the&#95;water&#95;cycle&#95;:6&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle_:6-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The hydrological cycle is a system whereby the evaporation of moisture in one place leads to precipitation (rain or snow) in another place. For example, evaporation always exceeds precipitation over the oceans. This allows moisture to be transported by the atmosphere from the oceans onto land where precipitation exceeds <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>. The runoff from the land flows into streams and rivers and discharges into the ocean, which completes the global cycle.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Effects&#95;of&#95;climate&#95;change&#95;on&#95;the&#95;water&#95;cycle&#95;:6&#95;39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle_:6-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The water cycle is a key part of <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_budget" title="Earth&#39;s energy budget">Earth's energy cycle</a> through the evaporative cooling at the surface which provides latent heat to the atmosphere, as atmospheric systems play a primary role in moving heat upward.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Effects&#95;of&#95;climate&#95;change&#95;on&#95;the&#95;water&#95;cycle&#95;:6&#95;39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle_:6-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Related_processes">Related processes</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Biogeochemical_cycling">Biogeochemical cycling</h3></div>
<p>While the water cycle is itself a <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical cycle</a>, flow of water over and beneath the Earth is a key component of the cycling of other biogeochemicals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Runoff is responsible for almost all of the transport of eroded <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> from land to <a href="/wiki/Waterbody" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterbody">waterbodies</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> of the oceans is derived from <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> and transport of dissolved salts from the land. Cultural <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> of lakes is primarily due to phosphorus, applied in excess to <a href="/wiki/Field_(agriculture)" title="Field (agriculture)">agricultural fields</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, and then transported overland and down rivers. Both runoff and groundwater flow play significant roles in transporting nitrogen from the land to waterbodies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dead_zone_(ecology)" title="Dead zone (ecology)">dead zone</a> at the outlet of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> is a consequence of <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a> from fertilizer being carried off agricultural fields and funnelled down the <a href="/wiki/River_system" class="mw-redirect" title="River system">river system</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>. Runoff also plays a part in the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, again through the transport of eroded rock and soil.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Slow_loss_over_geologic_time">Slow loss over geologic time</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_escape" title="Atmospheric escape">Atmospheric escape</a></div>
<p>The hydrodynamic wind within the upper portion of a planet's atmosphere allows light chemical elements such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">Hydrogen</a> to move up to the <a href="/wiki/Exobase" class="mw-redirect" title="Exobase">exobase</a>, the lower limit of the <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a>, where the gases can then reach <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a>, entering <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> without impacting other particles of gas. This type of gas loss from a planet into space is known as <a href="/wiki/Planetary_wind" class="mw-redirect" title="Planetary wind">planetary wind</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Planets with hot lower atmospheres could result in humid upper atmospheres that accelerate the loss of hydrogen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dvorak&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dvorak-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Historical_interpretations">Historical interpretations</h2></div>
<p>In ancient times, it was widely thought that the land mass floated on a body of water, and that most of the water in rivers has its origin under the earth. Examples of this belief can be found in the works of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;800 BCE</span>).
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Works_and_Days" title="Works and Days">Works and Days</a> (ca. 700 BC), the Greek poet Hesiod outlines the idea of the water cycle: "[Vapour] is drawn from the ever-flowing rivers and is raised high above the earth by windstorm, and sometimes it turns to rain towards evening, and sometimes to wind when Thracian Boreas huddles the thick clouds."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">ancient Near East</a>, Hebrew scholars observed that even though the rivers ran into the sea, the sea never became full. Some scholars conclude that the water cycle was described completely during this time in this passage: "The wind goeth toward the south, and turneth about unto the north; it whirleth about continually, and the wind returneth again according to its circuits. All the rivers run into the sea, yet the sea is not full; unto the place from whence the rivers come, thither they return again" (<a class="external text" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Ecclesiastes">Ecclesiastes 1:6-7</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Morris1988p.15&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morris1988p.15-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Furthermore, it was also observed that when the clouds were full, they emptied rain on the earth (<a class="external text" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Ecclesiastes#Chapter_11">Ecclesiastes 11:3</a>). 
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Adityahridayam" class="mw-redirect" title="Adityahridayam">Adityahridayam</a> (a devotional hymn to the Sun God) of <a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a>, a Hindu epic dated to the 4th century BCE, it is mentioned in the 22nd verse that the Sun heats up water and sends it down as rain. By roughly 500 BCE, Greek scholars were speculating that much of the water in rivers can be attributed to rain. The origin of rain was also known by then. These scholars maintained the belief, however, that water rising up through the earth contributed a great deal to rivers. Examples of this thinking included <a href="/wiki/Anaximander" title="Anaximander">Anaximander</a> (570 BCE) (who also speculated about the <a href="/wiki/History_of_evolutionary_thought" title="History of evolutionary thought">evolution of land animals from fish</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>) and <a href="/wiki/Xenophanes" title="Xenophanes">Xenophanes of Colophon</a> (530 BCE).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> Chinese scholars such as Chi Ni Tzu (320 BCE) and Lu Shih Ch'un Ch'iu (239 BCE) had similar thoughts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The idea that the water cycle is a closed cycle can be found in the works of <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras of Clazomenae</a> (460 BCE) and <a href="/wiki/Diogenes_of_Apollonia" title="Diogenes of Apollonia">Diogenes of Apollonia</a> (460 BCE). Both <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> (390 BCE) and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (350 BCE) speculated about percolation as part of the water cycle. Aristotle correctly hypothesized that the sun played a role in the Earth's hydraulic cycle in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Meteorology_(Aristotle)" title="Meteorology (Aristotle)">Meteorology</a>,</i> writing "By it [the sun's] agency the finest and sweetest water is everyday carried up and is dissolved into vapor and rises to the upper regions, where it is condensed again by the cold and so returns to the earth," and believed that clouds were composed of cooled and condensed water vapor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Much like the earlier Aristotle, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a> Chinese scientist <a href="/wiki/Wang_Chong" title="Wang Chong">Wang Chong</a> (27–100 AD) accurately described the water cycle <a href="/wiki/Geoscience" class="mw-redirect" title="Geoscience">of Earth</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Lunheng" title="Lunheng">Lunheng</a></i> but was dismissed by his contemporaries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Up to the time of the Renaissance, it was wrongly assumed that precipitation alone was insufficient to feed rivers, for a complete water cycle, and that underground water pushing upwards from the oceans were the main contributors to river water. <a href="/wiki/Bartholomeus_Anglicus" class="mw-redirect" title="Bartholomeus Anglicus">Bartholomew of England</a> held this view (1240 CE), as did Leonardo da Vinci (1500 CE) and <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_Kircher" title="Athanasius Kircher">Athanasius Kircher</a> (1644 CE).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Discovery_of_the_correct_theory">Discovery of the correct theory</h3></div>
<p>The first published thinker to assert that rainfall alone was sufficient for the maintenance of rivers was <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Palissy" title="Bernard Palissy">Bernard Palissy</a> (1580 CE), who is often credited as the discoverer of the modern theory of the water cycle. Palissy's theories were not tested scientifically until 1674, in a study commonly attributed to <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Perrault_(scientist)" title="Pierre Perrault (scientist)">Pierre Perrault</a>. Even then, these beliefs were not accepted in mainstream science until the early nineteenth century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
